class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Agriculture&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1337" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg/300px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg/450px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg/600px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ploughing with a yoke of horned cattle in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_agriculture" title="Ancient Egyptian agriculture">Ancient Egypt</a>. Painting from the burial chamber of <a href="/wiki/Sennedjem" title="Sennedjem">Sennedjem</a>, c. 1200 BC.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> began independently in different parts of the globe, and included a diverse range of <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a>. At least eleven separate regions of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> were involved as independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centers_of_origin" title="Centers of origin">centers of origin</a>.
The development of agriculture about 12,000 years ago changed the way humans lived. They switched from nomadic hunter-gatherer lifestyles to permanent settlements and farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wild <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a> were collected and eaten from at least 105,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup> However, domestication did not occur until much later. The earliest evidence of small-scale cultivation of edible grasses is from around 21,000 BC with the <a href="/wiki/Ohalo_II" title="Ohalo II">Ohalo II</a> people on the shores of the Sea of Galilee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> By around 9500 BC, the eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_founder_crops" title="Neolithic founder crops">Neolithic founder crops</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmer_wheat" title="Emmer wheat">emmer wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Einkorn_wheat" title="Einkorn wheat">einkorn wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">hulled barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vicia_ervilia" title="Vicia ervilia">bitter vetch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpeas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> – were cultivated in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">Rye</a> may have been cultivated earlier, but this claim remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">Rice</a> was domesticated in China by 6200 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1-6">[6]</a></sup> with earliest known cultivation from 5700 BC, followed by <a href="/wiki/Mung_bean" title="Mung bean">mung</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy">soy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azuki_bean" title="Azuki bean">azuki</a> beans. Rice was also independently domesticated in West Africa and cultivated by 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi_e1007414_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choi_e1007414-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cubry_2274–2282.e6_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cubry_2274–2282.e6-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">Pigs</a> were domesticated in Mesopotamia around 11,000 years ago, followed by <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> were domesticated from the wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> in the areas of modern Turkey and India around 8500 BC. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camels" title="Camels">Camels</a> were domesticated late, perhaps around 3000 BC.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">subsaharan Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">Sorghum</a> was domesticated in the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> region of Africa by 3000 BC, along with <a href="/wiki/Pearl_millet" title="Pearl millet">Pearl Millet</a> by 2000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yam_(vegetable)" title="Yam (vegetable)">Yams</a> were domesticated in several distinct locations, including West Africa (unknown date), and <a href="/wiki/Cowpea" title="Cowpea">Cowpeas</a> by 2500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">Rice</a> (<a href="/wiki/Oryza_glaberrima" title="Oryza glaberrima">African Rice</a>) was also independently domesticated in West Africa and cultivated by 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi_e1007414_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Choi_e1007414-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cubry_2274–2282.e6_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cubry_2274–2282.e6-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Teff" title="Teff">Teff</a> and likely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finger_millet" title="Finger millet">Finger Millet</a> were domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> by 3000 BC, along with <a href="/wiki/Guizotia_abyssinica" title="Guizotia abyssinica">Noog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ensete" title="Ensete">Ensete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coffee_bean" title="Coffee bean">Coffee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Other plant foods domesticated in Africa include <a href="/wiki/Watermelon" title="Watermelon">Watermelon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okra" title="Okra">Okra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamarind" title="Tamarind">Tamarind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black-eyed_pea" title="Black-eyed pea">black eyed peas</a>, along with tree crops such as the <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">Kola nut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elaeis" title="Elaeis">oil Palm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/True_plantains" title="True plantains">Plantains</a> were cultivated in Africa by 3000 BC and <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">Bananas</a> by 1500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>  The helmeted <a href="/wiki/Guineafowl" title="Guineafowl">Guinea fowl</a> was domesticated in West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sanga_cattle" title="Sanga cattle">Sanga Cattle</a> was likely also domesticated in North-East Africa, around 7000 BC, and later crossbred with other species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In South America, agriculture began as early as 9000 BC, starting with the cultivation of several species of plants that later became only minor crops. In the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> of South America, the <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> was domesticated between 8000 BC and 5000 BC, along with <a href="/wiki/Phaseolus" title="Phaseolus">beans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squash</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomatoes" title="Tomatoes">tomatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpacas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">guinea pigs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">Cassava</a> was domesticated in the Amazon Basin no later than 7000 BC. <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">Maize</a> (<i>Zea mays</i>) found its way to South America from <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, where wild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> was domesticated about 7000 BC and <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selectively bred</a> to become domestic maize. <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> by 4200 BC; another species of cotton was domesticated in Mesoamerica and became by far the most important species of cotton in the textile industry in modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Evidence of agriculture in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Agricultural_Complex" title="Eastern Agricultural Complex">Eastern</a> United States dates to about 3000 BCE. Several plants were cultivated, later to be replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(agriculture)" title="Three Sisters (agriculture)">Three Sisters</a> cultivation of maize, squash, and beans.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_root_vegetables" title="List of root vegetables">root vegetables</a> were domesticated in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> around 7000 BC. <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">Bananas</a> were cultivated and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridized</a> in the same period in <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>.  In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, agriculture was invented at a currently unspecified period, with the oldest eel traps of <a href="/wiki/Budj_Bim" title="Budj Bim">Budj Bim</a> dating to 6,600 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and the deployment of several crops ranging from yams<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gammage,_Bill_2011_pp._281_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gammage,_Bill_2011_pp._281-23">[23]</a></sup> to bananas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.com_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.com-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, from c. 3300 BC, witnessed the intensification of agriculture in civilizations such as <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerma" title="Kerma">ancient Sudan</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>. From 100 BC to 1600 AD, world population continued to grow along with land use, as evidenced by the rapid increase in <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a> from cattle and the cultivation of rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and era of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>, the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, both <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">the Republic</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">the Empire</a>, throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Mediterranean" title="Ancient Mediterranean">ancient Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> built upon existing systems of agriculture while also establishing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manorial" title="Manorial">manorial</a> system that became a bedrock of medieval agriculture. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, both in Europe and <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">in the Islamic world</a>, agriculture was transformed with improved techniques and the diffusion of crop plants, including the introduction of sugar, rice, cotton and fruit trees such as the <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">orange</a> to Europe by way of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">voyages of Christopher Columbus</a> in 1492, the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a> brought New World crops such as <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potatoes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">manioc</a> to Europe, and Old World crops such as wheat, barley, rice, and <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnips</a>, and livestock including horses, cattle, sheep, and goats to the Americas.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">Irrigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> were introduced soon after the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and developed much further in the past 200 years, starting with the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a>. Since 1900, agriculture in the developed nations, and to a lesser extent in the developing world, has seen large rises in productivity as human labour has been replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanized_farming" title="Mechanized farming">mechanization</a>, and assisted by synthetic fertilizers, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber-Bosch process</a> allowed the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a> fertilizer on an industrial scale, greatly increasing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_yields" title="Crop yields">crop yields</a>. Modern agriculture has raised social, political, and environmental issues including overpopulation, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farm_subsidies" title="Farm subsidies">farm subsidies</a>. In response, <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> developed in the twentieth century as an alternative to the use of synthetic pesticides.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_hypotheses">Origin hypotheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origin hypotheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout,_from_Australia_(1876,_vol_II).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="715" data-file-width="1363" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout%2C_from_Australia_%281876%2C_vol_II%29.jpg/220px-Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout%2C_from_Australia_%281876%2C_vol_II%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout%2C_from_Australia_%281876%2C_vol_II%29.jpg/330px-Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout%2C_from_Australia_%281876%2C_vol_II%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout%2C_from_Australia_%281876%2C_vol_II%29.jpg/440px-Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout%2C_from_Australia_%281876%2C_vol_II%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Native_Encampment_by_Skinner_Prout,_from_Australia_(1876,_vol_II).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australian" title="Indigenous Australian">Indigenous Australian</a> camp by Skinner Prout, 1876</div></div></div>
<p>Scholars have developed a number of hypotheses to explain the historical origins of agriculture. Studies of the transition from <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> to agricultural societies indicate an antecedent period of intensification and increasing <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentism</a>; examples are the <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian culture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, and the Early Chinese Neolithic in China. Current models indicate that wild stands that had been harvested previously started to be planted, but were not immediately domesticated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerritsen2008_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerritsen2008-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Localised climate change is the favoured explanation for the origins of agriculture in the Levant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeog_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeog-28">[28]</a></sup> When major climate change took place after the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a> (c. 11,000 BC), much of the earth became subject to long dry seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> These conditions favoured <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annual plants</a> which die off in the long dry season, leaving a <a href="/wiki/Dormancy" title="Dormancy">dormant</a> <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seed</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tuber" title="Tuber">tuber</a>. An abundance of readily storable wild grains and <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">pulses</a> enabled hunter-gatherers in some areas to form the first settled villages at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_development">Early development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_food_origins" title="List of food origins">List of food origins</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ClaySumerianSickle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/ClaySumerianSickle.jpg/220px-ClaySumerianSickle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/ClaySumerianSickle.jpg/330px-ClaySumerianSickle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/ClaySumerianSickle.jpg/440px-ClaySumerianSickle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ClaySumerianSickle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> harvester's sickle, 3000 BC, made from baked clay</div></div></div>
<p>Early people began altering communities of <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> for their own benefit through means such as <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">fire-stick farming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forest_gardening" title="Forest gardening">forest gardening</a> very early.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biggest_Estate_on_Earth,_Australia_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biggest_Estate_on_Earth,_Australia-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> 
Wild <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a> have been collected and eaten from at least 105,000 years ago, and possibly much longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup> Exact dates are hard to determine, as people collected and ate seeds before domesticating them, and plant characteristics may have changed during this period without human selection. An example is the semi-tough <a href="/wiki/Rachis" title="Rachis">rachis</a> and larger seeds of <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> from just after the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas" title="Younger Dryas">Younger Dryas</a> (about 9500 BC) in the early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> region of the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">Monophyletic</a> characteristics were attained without any human intervention, implying that apparent domestication of the cereal <a href="/wiki/Rachis" title="Rachis">rachis</a> could have occurred quite naturally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/220px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/330px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/440px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Indian farmer with a rock-weighted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scratch_plough" title="Scratch plough">scratch plough</a> pulled by two oxen. Similar ploughs were used throughout antiquity.</div></div></div>
<p>Agriculture began independently in different parts of the globe, and included a diverse range of taxa. At least 11 separate regions of the Old and New World were involved as independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centers_of_origin" title="Centers of origin">centers of origin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Some of the earliest known domestications were of animals. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_pig" title="Domestic pig">Domestic pigs</a> had multiple centres of origin in Eurasia, including Europe, East Asia and Southwest Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> where <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a> were first domesticated about 10,500 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">Sheep</a> were domesticated in Mesopotamia between 11,000 BC and 9000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> were domesticated from the wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> in the areas of modern Turkey and Pakistan around 8500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McTavish_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-39">[39]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camels" title="Camels">Camels</a> were domesticated late, perhaps around 3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sapir-Hen_2013_277–285_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sapir-Hen_2013_277–285-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg/300px-Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg/450px-Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg/600px-Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vavilov-centers_updated.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_origin" title="Center of origin">Centres of origin</a> identified by <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Vavilov" title="Nikolai Vavilov">Nikolai Vavilov</a> in the 1930s. Area 3 (grey) is no longer recognised as a centre of origin, and <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> (red, 'P') was identified more recently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>It was not until after 9500 BC that the eight so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_founder_crops" title="Neolithic founder crops">founder crops</a> of agriculture appear: first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmer_wheat" title="Emmer wheat">emmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Einkorn_wheat" title="Einkorn wheat">einkorn wheat</a>, then hulled <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vicia_ervilia" title="Vicia ervilia">bitter vetch</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chick_pea" title="Chick pea">chick peas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>. These eight crops occur more or less simultaneously on Pre-Pottery Neolithic B (<a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic_B" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic B">PPNB</a>) sites in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> was the first to be grown and harvested on a significant scale. At around the same time (9400 BC), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthenocarpic" title="Parthenocarpic">parthenocarpic</a> <a href="/wiki/Ficus" title="Ficus">fig</a> trees were domesticated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Domesticated <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> occurs in small quantities at some <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> sites in (Asia Minor) Turkey, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic_B" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic B">Pre-Pottery Neolithic B</a> (c. 7600 – c. 6000 BC) Can Hasan III near <a href="/wiki/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Çatalhöyük">Çatalhöyük</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillman_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hillman-44">[44]</a></sup> but is otherwise absent until the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> of central Europe, c. 1800–1500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZoharyHopf2012_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZoharyHopf2012-45">[45]</a></sup> Claims of much earlier cultivation of rye, at the <a href="/wiki/Epipalaeolithic" title="Epipalaeolithic">Epipalaeolithic</a> site of <a href="/wiki/Tell_Abu_Hureyra" title="Tell Abu Hureyra">Tell Abu Hureyra</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> valley of northern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, remain controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillman2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hillman2-46">[46]</a></sup> Critics point to inconsistencies in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiocarbon" title="Radiocarbon">radiocarbon</a> dates, and identifications based solely on grain, rather than on <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">chaff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sue_Colledge_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sue_Colledge-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 8000 BC, farming was entrenched on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. About this time, agriculture was developed independently in the Far East, probably in China, with rice rather than wheat as the primary crop. Maize was domesticated from the wild grass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> in southern Mexico by 6700 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> (8000 BC), <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Capsicum" title="Capsicum">pepper</a> (4000 BC), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(fruit)" title="Squash (fruit)">squash</a> (8000 BC) and several varieties of <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">bean</a> (8000 BC onwards) were domesticated in the New World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Agriculture was independently developed on the island of <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">Banana</a> cultivation of <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i>, including <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a>, dates back to 5000 BC, and possibly to 8000 BC, in <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">Bees were kept</a> for honey in the Middle East around 7000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9kya_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-9kya-54">[54]</a></sup> Archaeological evidence from various sites on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> suggest the domestication of plants and animals between 6000 and 4500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9ide_Fields" title="Céide Fields">Céide Fields</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, consisting of extensive tracts of land enclosed by stone walls, date to 3500 BC and are the oldest known field systems in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">horse was domesticated</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontic_steppe" title="Pontic steppe">Pontic steppe</a> around 4000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannabis" title="Cannabis">Cannabis</a> was in use in China in Neolithic times and may have been domesticated there; it was in use both as a fibre for ropemaking and as a medicine in Ancient Egypt by about 2350 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_bull_cart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2897" data-file-width="4345" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Old_bull_cart.jpg/220px-Old_bull_cart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Old_bull_cart.jpg/330px-Old_bull_cart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Old_bull_cart.jpg/440px-Old_bull_cart.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_bull_cart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clay and wood model of a bull cart carrying farm produce in large pots, <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a>. The site was abandoned in the 19th century BC.</div></div></div>
<p>In northern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> was domesticated by early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan" title="Sino-Tibetan">Sino-Tibetan</a> speakers at around 8000 to 6000 BC, becoming the main crop of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> basin by 5500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He2017_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-He2017-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lu_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lu-61">[61]</a></sup> They were followed by <a href="/wiki/Mung_bean" title="Mung bean">mung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azuki_bean" title="Azuki bean">azuki</a> beans.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="1053" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg/330px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg/495px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg/660px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chronological dispersal of <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chambers2013_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers2013-62">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In southern China, rice was domesticated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> basin at around 11,500 to 6200 BC, along with the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetland_agriculture" title="Wetland agriculture">wetland agriculture</a>, by early <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hmong-Mien_languages" title="Hmong-Mien languages">Hmong-Mien</a>-speakers. Other food plants were also harvested, including <a href="/wiki/Acorn" title="Acorn">acorns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eleocharis_dulcis" title="Eleocharis dulcis">water chestnuts</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foxnut" title="Foxnut">foxnuts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He2017_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-He2017-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellwood2011_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellwood2011-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hsieh2011_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hsieh2011-64">[64]</a></sup> Rice cultivation was later spread to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_Southeast_Asia" title="Island Southeast Asia">Island Southeast Asia</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_expansion" title="Austronesian expansion">Austronesian expansion</a>, starting at around 3,500 to 2,000 BC. This migration event also saw the introduction of cultivated and domesticated food plants from <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_Southeast_Asia" title="Island Southeast Asia">Island Southeast Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Islands" title="Pacific Islands">Pacific Islands</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canoe_plants" title="Canoe plants">canoe plants</a>. Contact with <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_India" title="Southern India">Southern India</a> by Austronesian sailors also led to an exchange of food plants which later became the origin of the valuable <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilboa2016_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilboa2016-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zumbroich2007_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zumbroich2007-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahdi1999_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahdi1999-67">[67]</a></sup> In the 1st millennium AD, Austronesian sailors also settled <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>, bringing Southeast Asian and South Asian food plants with them to the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East African</a> coast, including bananas and rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaujard2011_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beaujard2011-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WalterLebot2007_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-WalterLebot2007-69">[69]</a></sup> Rice was also spread southwards into <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a> by around 2000 to 1500 BC by the migrations of the early <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">Austroasiatic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kra-Dai" title="Kra-Dai">Kra-Dai</a>-speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellwood2011_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bellwood2011-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a> was domesticated by 3000 BC in Sudan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pearl_millet" title="Pearl millet">pearl millet</a> by 2500 BC in Mali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">Kola nut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> were also domesticated in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carney2011_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carney2011-72">[72]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papuan_people" title="Papuan people">Papuan peoples</a> began practicing agriculture around 7000 BC, domesticating <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taro" title="Taro">taro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2011_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2011-73">[73]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley" title="Indus Valley">Indus Valley</a> from the eighth millennium BC onwards at <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a>, 2-row and 6-row barley were cultivated, along with einkorn, emmer, and durum wheats, and dates. In the earliest levels of Merhgarh, wild game such as <a href="/wiki/Gazelle" title="Gazelle">gazelle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swamp_deer" title="Swamp deer">swamp deer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackbuck" title="Blackbuck">blackbuck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chital" title="Chital">chital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onager" title="Onager">wild ass</a>, wild goat, wild sheep, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boar" title="Boar">boar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nilgai" title="Nilgai">nilgai</a> were all hunted for food. These are successively replaced by domesticated sheep, goats, and humped <a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">zebu</a> cattle by the fifth millennium BC, indicating the gradual transition from hunting and gathering to agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barker2009_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barker2009-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">Maize</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squash</a> were domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>; <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> in South America, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflower" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a> in the Eastern Woodlands of North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonGoudie2013_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonGoudie2013-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilizations">Civilizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Civilizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sumer">Sumer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sumer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="1522" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg/220px-VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg/330px-VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg/440px-VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VAM_-_Rollsiegel_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Domesticated animals on a <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">cylinder seal</a>, 2500 BC</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> farmers grew the cereals <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, starting to live in villages from about 8000 BC. Given the low rainfall of the region, agriculture relied on the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> rivers. Irrigation canals leading from the rivers permitted the growth of cereals in large enough quantities to support cities. The first ploughs appear in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictograph" title="Pictograph">pictographs</a> from Uruk around 3000 BC; seed-ploughs that funneled seed into the ploughed furrow appear on <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seals</a> around 2300 BC. Vegetable crops included <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpeas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentils</a>, peas, beans, <a href="/wiki/Onion" title="Onion">onions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garlic" title="Garlic">garlic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lettuce" title="Lettuce">lettuce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mustard_plant" title="Mustard plant">mustard</a>. They grew fruits including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Date_(fruit)" title="Date (fruit)">dates</a>, grapes, apples, melons, and figs. Alongside their farming, Sumerians also caught fish and hunted <a href="/wiki/Fowl" title="Fowl">fowl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gazelle" title="Gazelle">gazelle</a>. The meat of sheep, goats, cows and poultry was eaten, mainly by the elite. Fish was preserved by drying, salting and smoking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritMus_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritMus-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egypt">Ancient Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ancient Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Nakht_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="828" data-file-width="630" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg/170px-Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg/255px-Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg/340px-Tomb_of_Nakht_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Nakht_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Agricultural scenes of <a href="/wiki/Threshing" title="Threshing">threshing</a>, a grain store, harvesting with <a href="/wiki/Sickle" title="Sickle">sickles</a>, digging, tree-cutting and ploughing from Ancient Egypt. Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Nakht" title="Nakht">Nakht</a>, 15th century BC.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_agriculture" title="Ancient Egyptian agriculture">Ancient Egyptian agriculture</a></div>
<p>The civilization of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> was indebted to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a> and its dependable seasonal flooding. The river's predictability and the fertile soil allowed the Egyptians to build an empire on the basis of great agricultural wealth. Egyptians were among the first peoples to practice agriculture on a large scale, starting in the pre-dynastic period from the end of the Paleolithic into the Neolithic, between around 10,000 BC and 4000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janick_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janick-78">[78]</a></sup> This was made possible with the development of basin irrigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Their staple food crops were grains such as wheat and barley, alongside industrial crops such as <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janick_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Janick-78">[78]</a></sup> Archaeological evidence also suggests that the spread of agriculture was facilitated by the influx of farming communities from the tropical Sahara 6,500 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Agriculture in the Indus Valley Civilisation">Agriculture in the Indus Valley Civilisation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Pakistan" title="Agriculture in Pakistan">Agriculture in Pakistan</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jujube" title="Jujube">Jujube</a> was domesticated in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> by 9000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-81">[81]</a></sup> Barley and wheat cultivation – along with the domestication of cattle, primarily sheep and goats – followed in <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh culture</a> by 8000–6000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baber_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baber-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrisandgosden385_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-harrisandgosden385-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_2009_p44_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wright_2009_p44-84">[84]</a></sup> This period also saw the first domestication of the <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_farming" title="Pastoral farming">Pastoral farming</a> in <a href="/wiki/India_(Herodotus)" title="India (Herodotus)">India</a> included threshing, planting crops in rows – either of two or of six – and storing grain in <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">granaries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrisandgosden385_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-harrisandgosden385-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Possehl_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Possehl-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was cultivated by the 5th–4th millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> By the 5th millennium BC, agricultural communities became widespread in <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrisandgosden385_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-harrisandgosden385-83">[83]</a></sup> Irrigation was developed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a> by around 4500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R&amp;U_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-R&amp;U-87">[87]</a></sup> The size and prosperity of the Indus civilization grew as a result of this innovation, leading to more thoroughly planned settlements which used <a href="/wiki/Drainage" title="Drainage">drainage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer" title="Sanitary sewer">sewers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R&amp;U_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-R&amp;U-87">[87]</a></sup> Archeological evidence of an animal-drawn <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">plough</a> dates back to 2500 BC in the Indus Valley Civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lal_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-lal-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_China">Ancient China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ancient China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_China" title="Agriculture in China">Agriculture in China</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agriculture_(Chinese_mythology)" title="Agriculture (Chinese mythology)">Agriculture (Chinese mythology)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg/220px-Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg/330px-Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg/440px-Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Terrace_field_yunnan_china.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_terrace" title="Rice terrace">rice terraces</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yuanyang_County,_Yunnan" title="Yuanyang County, Yunnan">Yuanyang County, Yunnan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Records from the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> provide a picture of early <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_China" title="Agriculture in China">Chinese agriculture</a> from the 5th century BC to 2nd century AD which included a nationwide <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">granary</a> system and widespread use of <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">sericulture</a>. An important early Chinese book on agriculture is the <a href="/wiki/Qimin_Yaoshu" title="Qimin Yaoshu">Qimin Yaoshu</a> of AD 535, written by Jia Sixie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_6_part_2_55_56_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_6_part_2_55_56-89">[89]</a></sup> Jia's writing style was straightforward and lucid relative to the elaborate and allusive writing typical of the time. Jia's book was also very long, with over one hundred thousand written <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, and it quoted many other Chinese books that were written previously, but no longer survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_6_part_2_56_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_6_part_2_56-90">[90]</a></sup> The contents of Jia's 6th century book include sections on land preparation, seeding, cultivation, orchard management, forestry, and animal husbandry. The book also includes peripherally related content covering trade and culinary uses for crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_6_part_2_57_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_6_part_2_57-91">[91]</a></sup> The work and the style in which it was written proved influential on later Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agronomist" title="Agronomist">agronomists</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wang_Zhen_(official)" title="Wang Zhen (official)">Wang Zhen</a> and his groundbreaking <i>Nong Shu</i> of 1313.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_6_part_2_56_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_6_part_2_56-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1580" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG/220px-Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG/330px-Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG/440px-Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Song_Dynasty_Hydraulic_Mill_for_Grain.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Northern Song era</a> (960–1127 AD) Chinese <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermill</a> for <a href="/wiki/Husk" title="Husk">dehusking</a> grain with a horizontal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterwheel" title="Waterwheel">waterwheel</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>For agricultural purposes, the Chinese had innovated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a>-powered <a href="/wiki/Trip_hammer" title="Trip hammer">trip hammer</a> by the 1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_184_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_184-92">[92]</a></sup> Although it found other purposes, its main function to pound, decorticate, and polish grain that otherwise would have been done manually. The Chinese also began using the square-pallet <a href="/wiki/Chain_pump" title="Chain pump">chain pump</a> by the 1st century AD, powered by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterwheel" title="Waterwheel">waterwheel</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxen" title="Oxen">oxen</a> pulling an on a system of mechanical wheels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_89_110_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_89_110-93">[93]</a></sup> Although the chain pump found use in <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">public works</a> of providing water for urban and palatial <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipe systems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_33_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_33-94">[94]</a></sup> it was used largely to lift water from a lower to higher elevation in filling <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Channel_(geography)" title="Channel (geography)">channels</a> for <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">farmland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_110_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_110-95">[95]</a></sup> By the end of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> in the late 2nd century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_plough" title="Heavy plough">heavy ploughs</a> had been developed with iron ploughshares and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mouldboard" title="Mouldboard">mouldboards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenberger_2006_11-12_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenberger_2006_11-12-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> These slowly spread west, revolutionizing farming in Northern Europe by the 10th century. (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Glick" title="Thomas Glick">Thomas Glick</a>, however, argues for a development of the Chinese plough as late as the 9th century, implying its spread east from similar designs known in Italy by the 7th century.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Asian rice was domesticated 8,200–13,500 years ago in China, with a single genetic origin from the wild rice <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_rufipogon" title="Oryza rufipogon">Oryza rufipogon</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1-6">[6]</a></sup> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_River_(China)" title="Pearl River (China)">Pearl River</a> valley region of China. Rice cultivation then spread to South and Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature1_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature1-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greece_and_Hellenistic_world">Ancient Greece and Hellenistic world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ancient Greece and Hellenistic world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Greece" title="Agriculture in ancient Greece">Agriculture in ancient Greece</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Noe_082.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="152" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Noe_082.jpg/220px-Noe_082.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Noe_082.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Noe_082.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ear of <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, symbol of wealth in the city of <a href="/wiki/Metapontum" title="Metapontum">Metapontum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a> (i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">Greek colonies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">southern Italy</a>), stamped <a href="/wiki/Stater" title="Stater">stater</a>, c. 530–510 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The major cereal crops of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Mediterranean" title="Ancient Mediterranean">ancient Mediterranean</a> region were wheat, <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a>, and barley, while common vegetables included peas, beans, <a href="/wiki/Vicia_faba" title="Vicia faba">fava</a>, and olives, dairy products came mostly from sheep and goats, and meat, which was consumed on rare occasion for most people, usually consisted of pork, beef, and lamb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koester_1995_p76-77_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-koester_1995_p76-77-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Greece" title="Agriculture in ancient Greece">Agriculture in ancient Greece</a> was hindered by the <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainland_Greece" title="Mainland Greece">mainland Greece</a> that only allowed for roughly 10% of the land to be cultivated properly, necessitating the specialized exportation of <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_wine" title="History of wine">wine</a> and importation of grains from <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> (centered in what is now <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bulgaria" title="History of Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">Greek colonies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greeks</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_dynasty" title="Ptolemaic dynasty">Ptolemaic Empire</a> controlled <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a>, major grain-producing regions that mainland <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> depended on for subsistence, while the Ptolemaic grain market also played a critical role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rise_of_Rome" title="Rise of Rome">the rise</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, Mesopotamia was a crucial area for the production of wheat, while <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a> was also practiced in other parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koester_1995_p77_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-koester_1995_p77-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Empire">Roman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Roman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_agriculture" title="Roman agriculture">Roman agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A4hmaschine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="246" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/M%C3%A4hmaschine.jpg/220px-M%C3%A4hmaschine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/M%C3%A4hmaschine.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A4hmaschine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_agriculture" title="Roman agriculture">Roman</a> harvesting machine, a <i>vallus</i>, from a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_defensive_walls" title="Ancient Roman defensive walls">Roman wall</a> in Belgium, which was then part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Belgica" title="Gallia Belgica">Gallia Belgica</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a> of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_agriculture" title="Roman agriculture">Roman agriculture</a> was built on techniques originally pioneered by the Sumerians, transmitted to them by subsequent cultures, with a specific emphasis on the cultivation of crops for trade and export. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">The Romans</a> laid the groundwork for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manorial" title="Manorial">manorial</a> economic system, involving <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>, which flourished in the Middle Ages. The farm sizes in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Rome" title="History of Rome">Rome</a> can be divided into three categories. Small farms were from 18 to 88 iugera (one iugerum is equal to about 0.65 acre). Medium-sized farms were from 80 to 500 iugera (singular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iugerum" title="Iugerum">iugerum</a>). Large estates (called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latifundia" title="Latifundia">latifundia</a>) were over 500 iugera. <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">The Romans</a> had four systems of farm management: direct work by owner and his family; slaves doing work under supervision of slave managers; <a href="/wiki/Tenant_farmer" title="Tenant farmer">tenant farming</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sharecropping" title="Sharecropping">sharecropping</a> in which the owner and a tenant divide up a farm's produce; and situations in which a farm was leased to a tenant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-White-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Americas">The Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Agricultural history took a different path from the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> as the Americas lacked large-seeded, easily domesticated grains (such as wheat and barley) and large domestic animals that could be used for agricultural labor. Rather than the practice which developed in the Old World of sowing a field with a single crop, pre-historic American agriculture usually consisted of cultivating many crops in close proximity to each other utilizing only hand labor. Moreover, agricultural areas in the Americas lacked the uniformity of the east–west area of <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-arid climates</a> in southern Europe and southwestern Asia, but instead had a north–south pattern with a variety of different climatic zones which fostered the domestication of many different plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diamond-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the time of first contact between the Europeans and the Americans, the Europeans practiced "extensive agriculture, based on the plough and draught animals," with tenants under landlords, but also forced labor or slavery, while the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> practiced "intensive agriculture, based on human labour."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sempat_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sempat-104">[104]</a></sup> Europeans wanted control of land for the grazing of their livestock and property rights for the control of production. Though they were impressed with the productivity of traditional farming techniques, they saw no connection to their system and were dismissive of Native American practices as "gardening" rather than a commercializable enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sempat_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sempat-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill-105">[105]</a></sup> The hemisphere's most important crop, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, became more productive than Old World grain crops, after several thousand years of selective breeding, producing two and one half times more calories per acre than wheat and barley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Incan_agriculture" title="Incan agriculture">Incan agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture_in_Chile" title="History of agriculture in Chile">History of agriculture in Chile</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1463" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg/250px-Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg/375px-Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg/500px-Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colca_Canyon_Puno.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Agriculture terraces were (and are) common in the austere, high-elevation environment of the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trabajo-inca8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="414" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Trabajo-inca8.jpg/170px-Trabajo-inca8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Trabajo-inca8.jpg/255px-Trabajo-inca8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Trabajo-inca8.jpg/340px-Trabajo-inca8.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trabajo-inca8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Incan_agriculture" title="Incan agriculture">Inca farmers</a> using a human-powered <a href="/wiki/Foot_plough" title="Foot plough">foot plough</a></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest known areas of possible agriculture in the Americas dating to about 9000 BC are in <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, near present-day <a href="/wiki/Pereira,_Colombia" title="Pereira, Colombia">Pereira</a>, and by the <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_culture_(archaeology)" title="Las Vegas culture (archaeology)">Las Vegas</a> culture in <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Elena_Province" title="Santa Elena Province">Santa Elena peninsula</a>. The plants cultivated (or manipulated by humans) were <a href="/wiki/Goeppertia_allouia" title="Goeppertia allouia">leren</a> (<i>Calathea allouia</i>), <a href="/wiki/Maranta_arundinacea" title="Maranta arundinacea">arrowroot</a> (<i>Maranta arundinacea</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squash</a> (<i>Cucurbita</i> species), and <a href="/wiki/Calabash" title="Calabash">bottle gourd</a> (<i>Lagenaria siceraria</i>). All are plants of humid climates and their existence at this time on the semi-arid Santa Elena peninsula may be evidence that they were transplanted there from more humid environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zarillo_et_al_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zarillo_et_al-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piperno_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piperno-108">[108]</a></sup> In another study, this area of South America was identified as one of the four oldest places of origin for agriculture, along with the Fertile Crescent, China, and Mesoamerica, dated between 6200 BC and 10000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larson-109">[109]</a></sup> (To facilitate comprehension by readers, <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon calibrated BP</a> dates in the above sources have been converted to BC.)
</p><p>In the Andes region, with civilizations including the <a href="/wiki/Incan_agriculture" title="Incan agriculture">Inca</a>, the major crop was the <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a>, domesticated between 8000 and 5000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spooner_2005_14694–99_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spooner_2005_14694–99-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-online-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Michael_Francis_2005_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Michael_Francis_2005-112">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">Coca</a>, still a major crop to this day, was domesticated in the Andes, as were the <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2011_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2011-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was domesticated in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> by 4200 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broudy1979_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broudy1979-114">[114]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Incan_animal_husbandry" title="Incan animal husbandry">Animals were also domesticated</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llamas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpacas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">guinea pigs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RischkowskyPilling2007_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-RischkowskyPilling2007-115">[115]</a></sup> The people of the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> of South America grew large surpluses of food which they stored in buildings called <a href="/wiki/Qullqa" title="Qullqa">Qullqas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mumford_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mumford-116">[116]</a></sup>
