</p><p>The most important crop domesticated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin">Amazon Basin</a> and tropical lowlands was probably <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a>, (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manihot_esculenta" title="Manihot esculenta">Manihot esculenta</a>), which was domesticated before 7000 BCE, likely in the <a href="/wiki/Rond%C3%B4nia" title="Rondônia">Rondônia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mato_Grosso" title="Mato Grosso">Mato Grosso</a> provinces of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isendahl_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isendahl-117">[117]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Guaitecas_Archipelago" title="Guaitecas Archipelago">Guaitecas Archipelago</a> in modern Chile was the southern limit of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Hispanic" title="Pre-Hispanic">Pre-Hispanic</a> agriculture near 44° South latitude,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> as noted by the mention of the cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Potatoes_of_Chilo%C3%A9" title="Potatoes of Chiloé">Chiloé potatoes</a> by a Spanish expedition in 1557.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Contreras_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Contreras-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mesoamerica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Mesoamerica" title="Agriculture in Mesoamerica">Agriculture in Mesoamerica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1037" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/220px-Maize-teosinte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/330px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Maize-teosinte.jpg/440px-Maize-teosinte.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maize-teosinte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The creation of maize from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> (top), maize-teosinte hybrid (middle), to maize (bottom)</div></div></div>
<p>In Mesoamerica, wild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teosinte" title="Teosinte">teosinte</a> was transformed through human selection into the ancestor of modern maize, about 7,000 BC. It gradually spread across North America and to South America and was the most important crop of Native Americans at the time of European exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Other Mesoamerican crops include hundreds of varieties of locally domesticated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Theobroma_cacao" title="Theobroma cacao">cocoa</a>, also domesticated in the region, was a major crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murphy2011_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2011-73">[73]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(bird)" title="Turkey (bird)">turkey</a>, one of the most important meat birds, was probably domesticated in Mexico or the U.S. Southwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Speller_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Speller-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> were active farmers and had an agriculturally focused economy. The land around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Texcoco" title="Lake Texcoco">Lake Texcoco</a> was fertile, but not large enough to produce the amount of food needed for the population of their expanding empire. The Aztecs developed irrigation systems, formed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrace_(agriculture)" title="Terrace (agriculture)">terraced</a> hillsides, fertilized their soil, and developed <a href="/wiki/Chinampa" title="Chinampa">chinampas</a> or artificial islands, also known as "floating gardens". The <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayas</a> between 400 BC to 900 AD used extensive canal and raised field systems to farm swampland on the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture_in_the_United_States" title="History of agriculture in the United States">History of agriculture in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Agricultural_Complex" title="Eastern Agricultural Complex">Eastern Agricultural Complex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_agriculture_on_the_Great_Plains" title="Prehistoric agriculture on the Great Plains">Prehistoric agriculture on the Great Plains</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_prehistoric_Southwestern_United_States" title="Agriculture in the prehistoric Southwestern United States">Agriculture in the prehistoric Southwestern United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="822" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg/200px-Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg/300px-Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg/400px-Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wichita_Indian_village_1850-1875.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wichita_people" title="Wichita people">Wichita</a> village of grass houses surrounded by maize fields in the United States.</div></div></div>
<p>The indigenous people of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Agricultural_Complex" title="Eastern Agricultural Complex">Eastern U.S. domesticated numerous crops</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflowers" title="Sunflowers">Sunflowers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> varieties of squash and <i><a href="/wiki/Chenopodium" title="Chenopodium">Chenopodium</a></i>, as well as crops no longer grown, including <a href="/wiki/Iva_annua" title="Iva annua">marsh elder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hordeum_pusillum" title="Hordeum pusillum">little barley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Wild foods including <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maple_sugar" title="Maple sugar">maple sugar</a> were harvested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2013_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2013-127">[127]</a></sup> The domesticated <a href="/wiki/Strawberry" title="Strawberry">strawberry</a> is a hybrid of a Chilean and a North American species, developed by breeding in Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Two major crops, <a href="/wiki/Pecan" title="Pecan">pecans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concord_grape" title="Concord grape">Concord grapes</a>, were utilized extensively in prehistoric times but do not appear to have been domesticated until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_prehistoric_Southwest" title="Agriculture in the prehistoric Southwest">indigenous people in what is now California</a> and the Pacific Northwest practiced various forms of <a href="/wiki/Forest_gardening" title="Forest gardening">forest gardening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">fire-stick farming</a> in the forests, grasslands, mixed woodlands, and wetlands, ensuring that desired food and medicine plants continued to be available. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_use_of_fire" title="Native American use of fire">natives controlled fire</a> on a regional scale to create a low-intensity <a href="/wiki/Fire_ecology" title="Fire ecology">fire ecology</a> which prevented larger, catastrophic fires and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustained a low-density agriculture</a> in loose rotation; a sort of "wild" <a href="/wiki/Permaculture" title="Permaculture">permaculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunningham2010_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham2010-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>A system of <a href="/wiki/Companion_planting" title="Companion planting">companion planting</a> called <a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(agriculture)" title="Three Sisters (agriculture)">the Three Sisters</a> was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agriculture_on_the_prehistoric_Great_Plains" title="Agriculture on the prehistoric Great Plains">developed in North America</a>. Three crops that complemented each other were planted together: <a href="/wiki/Winter_squash" title="Winter squash">winter squash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (corn), and climbing <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a> (typically <a href="/wiki/Phaseolus_acutifolius" title="Phaseolus acutifolius">tepary beans</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phaseolus_vulgaris" title="Phaseolus vulgaris">common beans</a>). The maize provides a structure for the beans to climb, eliminating the need for poles. The beans provide the <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> to the soil that the other plants use, and the squash spreads along the ground, blocking the <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, helping prevent the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weeds</a>. The squash leaves also act as a "living <a href="/wiki/Mulch" title="Mulch">mulch</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landon_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-landon-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2208" data-file-width="1986" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg/170px-Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg/255px-Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg/340px-Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panicum_decompositum_habitus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Native millet, <i><a href="/wiki/Panicum_decompositum" title="Panicum decompositum">Panicum decompositum</a></i>, was planted and harvested by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a> in eastern central Australia.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">Fire-stick farming</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_Emu" title="Dark Emu">Dark Emu</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Indigenous Australians</a> were predominately nomadic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a>. Due to the policy of <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_nullius" title="Terra nullius">terra nullius</a></i>, Aboriginals were regarded as not having been capable of sustained agriculture. However, the current consensus is that various agricultural methods were employed by the indigenous people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gammage,_Bill_2011_pp._281_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gammage,_Bill_2011_pp._281-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.com_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.com-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In two regions of Central Australia, the central west coast and eastern central Australia, forms of agriculture were practiced. People living in permanent settlements of over 200 residents sowed or planted on a large scale and stored the harvested food. The Nhanda and Amangu of the central west coast grew yams (<i><a href="/wiki/Dioscorea" title="Dioscorea">Dioscorea hastifolia</a></i>), while various groups in eastern central Australia (the Corners Region) planted and harvested bush onions (<i>yaua</i> – <i><a href="/wiki/Cyperus_bulbosus" title="Cyperus bulbosus">Cyperus bulbosus</a></i>), native millet (<i>cooly, tindil</i> – <i><a href="/wiki/Panicum_decompositum" title="Panicum decompositum">Panicum decompositum</a></i>) and a <a href="/wiki/Sporocarp_(ferns)" title="Sporocarp (ferns)">sporocarp</a>, <i>ngardu</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Marsilea_drummondii" title="Marsilea drummondii">Marsilea drummondii</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biggest_Estate_on_Earth,_Australia_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biggest_Estate_on_Earth,_Australia-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 281–304">: 281–304 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerritsen2008_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gerritsen2008-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Indigenous Australians used systematic burning, <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">fire-stick farming</a>, to enhance natural productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> In the 1970s and 1980s archaeological research in south west Victoria established that the <a href="/wiki/Gunditjmara" title="Gunditjmara">Gunditjmara</a> and other groups had developed sophisticated eel farming and fish trapping systems over a period of nearly 5,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Harry_Lourandos" title="Harry Lourandos">Harry Lourandos</a> suggested in the 1980s that there was evidence of 'intensification' in progress across Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> a process that appeared to have continued through the preceding 5,000 years. These concepts led the historian <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gammage" title="Bill Gammage">Bill Gammage</a> to argue that in effect the whole continent was a managed landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biggest_Estate_on_Earth,_Australia_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Biggest_Estate_on_Earth,_Australia-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Torres Islanders are now known to have planted <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.com_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.com-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages_and_Early_Modern_period">Middle Ages and Early Modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Middle Ages and Early Modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Agriculture in the Middle Ages">Agriculture in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> saw further improvements in agriculture. <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">Monasteries</a> spread throughout <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and became important centers for the collection of knowledge related to agriculture and forestry. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manorial_system" title="Manorial system">manorial system</a> allowed large landowners to control their land and its laborers, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serf" title="Serf">serfs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> During the medieval period, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> was critical in the exchange of crops and technology between the European, Asia and African continents. Besides transporting numerous crops, they introduced the concept of summer irrigation to Europe and developed the beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantation</a> system of <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> growing through the use of slaves for intensive cultivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crescenzi_calendar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="833" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Crescenzi_calendar.jpg/220px-Crescenzi_calendar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Crescenzi_calendar.jpg/330px-Crescenzi_calendar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Crescenzi_calendar.jpg/440px-Crescenzi_calendar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crescenzi_calendar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Agricultural calendar, c. 1470, from a manuscript of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pietro_de_Crescenzi" title="Pietro de Crescenzi">Pietro de Crescenzi</a></div></div></div>
<p>By AD 900, developments in <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">iron smelting</a> allowed for increased production in Europe, leading to developments in the production of agricultural implements such as <a href="/wiki/Plough" title="Plough">ploughs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hand_tool" title="Hand tool">hand tools</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_shoe" title="Horse shoe">horse shoes</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Carruca" title="Carruca">carruca</a> heavy plough improved on the earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scratch_plough" title="Scratch plough">scratch plough</a>, with the adoption of the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mouldboard_plough" title="Mouldboard plough">mouldboard plough</a> to turn over the heavy, wet soils of northern Europe. This led to the clearing of northern European forests and an increase in agricultural production, which in turn led to an increase in population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> At the same time, some farmers in Europe moved from a two field <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> to a three field crop rotation in which one field of three was left fallow every year. This resulted in increased productivity and nutrition, as the change in rotations permitted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen-fixing" title="Nitrogen-fixing">nitrogen-fixing</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legumes" title="Legumes">legumes</a> such as peas, lentils and beans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Improved <a href="/wiki/Horse_harness" title="Horse harness">horse harnesses</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Whippletree_(mechanism)" title="Whippletree (mechanism)">whippletree</a> further improved cultivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">Watermills</a> were introduced by the Romans, but were improved throughout the Middle Ages, along with <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmills</a>, and used to grind grains into flour, to cut wood and to process flax and wool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Crops included wheat, <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a>, barley and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oats" title="Oats">oats</a>. Peas, beans, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vetches" title="Vetches">vetches</a> became common from the 13th century onward as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fodder_crop" title="Fodder crop">fodder crop</a> for animals and also for their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen-fixation" title="Nitrogen-fixation">nitrogen-fixation</a> fertilizing properties. Crop yields peaked in the 13th century, and stayed more or less steady until the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell1_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell1-148">[148]</a></sup> Though the limitations of medieval farming were once thought to have provided a ceiling for the population growth in the Middle Ages, recent studies have shown that the technology of medieval agriculture was always sufficient for the needs of the people under normal circumstances,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stone1_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stone1-150">[150]</a></sup> and that it was only during exceptionally harsh times, such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%931317" title="Great Famine of 1315–1317">terrible weather of 1315–17</a>, that the needs of the population could not be met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Langdon_2010_20–23_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Langdon_2010_20–23-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_world">Arab world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Arab world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2063" data-file-width="1386" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg/170px-Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg/255px-Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg/340px-Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_Wheel_of_Hama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Noria" title="Noria">Noria</a> wheels to lift water for irrigation and household use were among the technologies introduced to Europe via <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">medieval Islamic world</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<p>From the 8th century to the 14th century, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic world</a> underwent a transformation in agricultural practice, described by the historian Andrew Watson as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_agricultural_revolution" title="Arab agricultural revolution">Arab agricultural revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-153">[153]</a></sup> This transformation was driven by a number of factors including the diffusion of many crops and plants along Muslim trade routes, the spread of more advanced farming techniques, and an agricultural-economic system which promoted increased yields and efficiency. The shift in agricultural practice changed the economy, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_distribution" title="Population distribution">population distribution</a>, vegetation cover, agricultural production, population levels, <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urban growth</a>, the distribution of the labour force, cooking, diet, and clothing across the Islamic world. Muslim traders covered much of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, and trade enabled the diffusion of many crops, plants and farming techniques across the region, as well as the adaptation of crops, plants and techniques from beyond the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-153">[153]</a></sup> This diffusion introduced major crops to Europe by way of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>, along with the techniques for their cultivation and cuisine. Sugar cane, rice, and cotton were among the major crops transferred, along with <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">citrus</a> and other fruit trees, nut trees, vegetables such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aubergine" title="Aubergine">aubergine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spinach" title="Spinach">spinach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chard" title="Chard">chard</a>, and the use of imported spices such as <a href="/wiki/Cumin" title="Cumin">cumin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coriander" title="Coriander">coriander</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutmeg" title="Nutmeg">nutmeg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>. Intensive irrigation, <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>, and agricultural manuals were widely adopted. Irrigation, partly based on Roman technology, made use of <a href="/wiki/Noria" title="Noria">noria</a> water wheels, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_mill" title="Water mill">water mills</a>, dams and reservoirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeographic2015_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeographic2015-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbian_exchange">Columbian exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Columbian exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a></div><p>
After 1492, a <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">global exchange</a> of previously local crops and livestock breeds occurred. Maize, potatoes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet_potatoes" title="Sweet potatoes">sweet potatoes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">manioc</a> were the key crops that spread from the New World to the Old, while varieties of wheat, barley, rice and <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnips</a> traveled from the Old World to the New. There had been few livestock species in the New World, with horses, cattle, sheep and goats being completely unknown before their arrival with Old World settlers. Crops moving in both directions across the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> caused population growth around the world and a lasting effect on many cultures in the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">Early Modern period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4370" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder-_The_Harvesters_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvesters_(painting)" title="The Harvesters (painting)">The Harvesters</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder" title="Pieter Bruegel the Elder">Pieter Bruegel</a> – 1565</div></div></div>
<p>Maize and cassava were introduced from Brazil into Africa by Portuguese traders in the 16th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> becoming staple foods, replacing native African crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> After its introduction from South America to Spain in the late 1500s, the potato became a staple crop throughout Europe by the late 1700s. The potato allowed farmers to produce more food, and initially added variety to the European diet. The increased supply of food reduced disease, increased births and reduced mortality, causing a population boom throughout the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, the US and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman,_Jeff_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman,_Jeff-159">[159]</a></sup> The introduction of the potato also brought about the first intensive use of fertilizer, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a> imported to Europe from Peru, and the first artificial pesticide, in the form of an <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> compound used to fight <a href="/wiki/Colorado_potato_beetle" title="Colorado potato beetle">Colorado potato beetles</a>. Before the adoption of the potato as a major crop, the dependence on grain had caused repetitive regional and national famines when the crops failed, including 17 major famines in England between 1523 and 1623. The resulting dependence on the potato however caused the <a href="/wiki/European_Potato_Failure" title="European Potato Failure">European Potato Failure</a>, a disastrous crop failure from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potato_blight" title="Potato blight">disease</a> that resulted in widespread <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">famine and the death of over one million people in Ireland alone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mann,_Charles_C_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mann,_Charles_C-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_agriculture">Modern agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_agricultural_revolution">British agricultural revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: British agricultural revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_agricultural_machinery" title="List of agricultural machinery">List of agricultural machinery</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanized_agriculture" title="Mechanized agriculture">Mechanized agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Townshend,_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Charles_Townshend%2C_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt_%282%29.jpg/170px-Charles_Townshend%2C_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Charles_Townshend%2C_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt_%282%29.jpg/255px-Charles_Townshend%2C_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Charles_Townshend%2C_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt_%282%29.jpg/340px-Charles_Townshend%2C_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Townshend,_2nd_Viscount_Townshend_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The agriculturalist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Townshend,_2nd_Viscount_Townshend" title="Charles Townshend, 2nd Viscount Townshend">Charles 'Turnip' Townshend</a> introduced four-field <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> and the cultivation of turnips.</div></div></div>
<p>Between the 17th century and the mid-19th century, Britain saw a large increase in agricultural productivity and net output. New agricultural practices like <a href="/wiki/Enclosure" title="Enclosure">enclosure</a>, mechanization, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four-field_crop_rotation" title="Four-field crop rotation">four-field crop rotation</a> to maintain soil nutrients, and <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> enabled an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malthusian_trap" title="Malthusian trap">unprecedented population growth</a> to 5.7 million in 1750, freeing up a significant percentage of the workforce, and thereby helped drive the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. The productivity of wheat went up from 19 US bushels (670 l; 150 US dry gal; 150 imp gal) per <a href="/wiki/Acre" title="Acre">acre</a> in 1720 to around 30 US bushels (1,100 l; 240 US dry gal; 230 imp gal) by 1840, marking a major turning point in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_(1762).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1262" data-file-width="774" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_%281762%29.png/170px-Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_%281762%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_%281762%29.png/255px-Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_%281762%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_%281762%29.png/340px-Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_%281762%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jethro_Tull_seed_drill_(1762).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jethro_Tull_(agriculturist)" title="Jethro Tull (agriculturist)">Jethro Tull</a>'s seed drill, invented in 1701</div></div></div>
<p>Advice on more productive techniques for farming began to appear in England in the mid-17th century, from writers such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hartlib" title="Samuel Hartlib">Samuel Hartlib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Blith" title="Walter Blith">Walter Blith</a> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> The main problem in sustaining agriculture in one place for a long time was the depletion of nutrients, most importantly nitrogen levels, in the soil. To allow the soil to regenerate, productive land was often let fallow and in some places <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> was used. The Dutch four-field rotation system was popularised by the British agriculturist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Townshend,_2nd_Viscount_Townshend" title="Charles Townshend, 2nd Viscount Townshend">Charles Townshend</a> in the 18th century. The system (wheat, turnips, barley and clover), opened up a fodder crop and grazing crop allowing livestock to be bred year-round. The use of clover was especially important as the legume roots replenished soil nitrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
The mechanisation and rationalisation of agriculture was another important factor. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Bakewell_(farmer)" title="Robert Bakewell (farmer)">Robert Bakewell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Coke,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester_(seventh_creation)" title="Thomas Coke, 1st Earl of Leicester (seventh creation)">Thomas Coke</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>, and initiated a process of inbreeding to maximise desirable traits from the mid 18th century, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Leicester" title="New Leicester">New Leicester</a> sheep. Machines were invented to improve the efficiency of various agricultural operation, such as <a href="/wiki/Jethro_Tull_(agriculturist)" title="Jethro Tull (agriculturist)">Jethro Tull</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Seed_drill" title="Seed drill">seed drill</a> of 1701 that mechanised seeding at the correct depth and spacing and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Meikle" title="Andrew Meikle">Andrew Meikle</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Threshing_machine" title="Threshing machine">threshing machine</a> of 1784. Ploughs were steadily improved, from Joseph Foljambe's <a href="/wiki/Plough#Heavy_Ploughs" title="Plough">Rotherham iron plough</a> in 1730<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/James_Small_(inventor)" title="James Small (inventor)">James Small</a>'s improved "Scots Plough" metal in 1763. In 1789 <a href="/wiki/Ransomes,_Sims_%26_Jefferies" title="Ransomes, Sims &amp; Jefferies">Ransomes, Sims &amp; Jefferies</a> was producing 86 plough models for different soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Powered farm machinery began with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Trevithick" title="Richard Trevithick">Richard Trevithick</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Stationary_steam_engine" title="Stationary steam engine">stationary steam engine</a>, used to drive a threshing machine, in 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodge_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hodge-166">[166]</a></sup> Mechanisation spread to other farm uses through the 19th century. The first petrol-driven <a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">tractor</a> was built in America by <a href="/wiki/John_Froelich" title="John Froelich">John Froelich</a> in 1892.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Bennet_Lawes" title="John Bennet Lawes">John Bennet Lawes</a> began the scientific investigation of fertilization at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rothamsted_Experimental_Station" title="Rothamsted Experimental Station">Rothamsted Experimental Station</a> in 1843. He investigated the impact of inorganic and organic fertilizers on crop yield and founded one of the first artificial fertilizer manufacturing factories in 1842. Fertilizer, in the shape of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">sodium nitrate</a> deposits in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, was imported to Britain by <a href="/wiki/John_Thomas_North" title="John Thomas North">John Thomas North</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a> (birds droppings). The first commercial process for fertilizer production was the obtaining of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_phosphate" title="Super phosphate">phosphate</a> from the dissolution of <a href="/wiki/Coprolite" title="Coprolite">coprolites</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphuric_acid" title="Sulphuric acid">sulphuric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" title="Intensive agriculture">Intensive agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agriculture_(Plowing)_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="969" data-file-width="1379" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Agriculture_%28Plowing%29_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg/220px-Agriculture_%28Plowing%29_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Agriculture_%28Plowing%29_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg/330px-Agriculture_%28Plowing%29_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Agriculture_%28Plowing%29_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg/440px-Agriculture_%28Plowing%29_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agriculture_(Plowing)_CNE-v1-p58-H.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 20th-century image of a <a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">tractor</a> ploughing an <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a> field</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dan_Albone" title="Dan Albone">Dan Albone</a> constructed the first commercially successful gasoline-powered general purpose tractor in 1901, and the 1923 <a href="/wiki/International_Harvester" title="International Harvester">International Harvester</a> <a href="/wiki/Farmall" title="Farmall">Farmall</a> tractor marked a major point in the replacement of draft animals (particularly horses) with machines. Since that time, self-propelled mechanical harvesters (<a href="/wiki/Combine_harvester" title="Combine harvester">combines</a>), <a href="/wiki/Planter_(farm_implement)" title="Planter (farm implement)">planters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transplanter" title="Transplanter">transplanters</a> and other equipment have been developed, further revolutionizing agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> These inventions allowed farming tasks to be done with a speed and on a scale previously impossible, leading modern farms to output much greater volumes of high-quality produce per land unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bt_plants.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1302" data-file-width="715" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bt_plants.png/130px-Bt_plants.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bt_plants.png/195px-Bt_plants.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bt_plants.png/260px-Bt_plants.png 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bt_plants.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">Bt-toxins</a> in <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified</a> <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a> leaves (bottom) protect from damage by <a href="/wiki/European_corn_borer" title="European corn borer">corn borers</a> (top).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber-Bosch method</a> for synthesizing <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">ammonium nitrate</a> represented a major breakthrough and allowed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_yields" title="Crop yields">crop yields</a> to overcome previous constraints. It was first patented by German chemist <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Haber" title="Fritz Haber">Fritz Haber</a>. In 1910 <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bosch" title="Carl Bosch">Carl Bosch</a>, while working for German chemical company <a href="/wiki/BASF" title="BASF">BASF</a>, successfully commercialized the process and secured further patents. In the years after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the use of synthetic fertilizer increased rapidly, in sync with the increasing world population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Collective_farming" title="Collective farming">Collective farming</a> was widely practiced in the Soviet Union, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> countries, China, and Vietnam, starting in the 1930s in the Soviet Union; one result was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_famine_of_1932%E2%80%9333" title="Soviet famine of 1932–33">Soviet famine of 1932–33</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Another consequence occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Leap_Forward" title="Great Leap Forward">Great Leap Forward</a> in China initiated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mao_Tse-tung" title="Mao Tse-tung">Mao Tse-tung</a> that resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Chinese_Famine" title="Great Chinese Famine">Great Chinese Famine</a> from 1959 to 1961 and ultimately reshaped the thinking of <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a>.
</p><p>In the past century agriculture has been characterized by increased productivity, the substitution of synthetic fertilizers and pesticides for labour, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farm_subsidies" title="Farm subsidies">farm subsidies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Other applications of scientific research since 1950 in agriculture include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_manipulation" title="Gene manipulation">gene manipulation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_1996_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-James_1996-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fray_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fray-177">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hydroponics" title="Hydroponics">hydroponics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Douglas1975_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Douglas1975-178">[178]</a></sup> and the development of economically viable <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP2009_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP2009-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of people involved in farming in industrial countries fell radically from 24 percent of the American population to 1.5 percent in 2002. The number of farms also decreased and their ownership became more concentrated; for example, between 1967 and 2002, one million pig farms in America consolidated into 114,000, with 80 percent of the production on factory farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dominion_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-dominion-180">[180]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Worldwatch_Institute" title="Worldwatch Institute">Worldwatch Institute</a>, 74 percent of the world's poultry, 43 percent of beef, and 68 percent of eggs are produced this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dominion_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-dominion-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW2006_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-WW2006-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Famines however continued to sweep the globe through the 20th century. Through the effects of climatic events, government policy, war and crop failure, millions of people died in each of at least ten famines between the 1920s and the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smh.com.au_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-smh.com.au-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_Revolution">Green Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Green Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Norman_Borlaug.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="496" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Norman_Borlaug.jpg/220px-Norman_Borlaug.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Norman_Borlaug.jpg/330px-Norman_Borlaug.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Norman_Borlaug.jpg/440px-Norman_Borlaug.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Norman_Borlaug.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a>, father of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> of the 1970s, is credited with saving over a billion people worldwide from starvation.</div></div></div>
<p>The Green Revolution was a series of research, development, and <a href="/wiki/Technology_transfer" title="Technology transfer">technology transfer</a> initiatives, between the 1940s and the late 1970s. It increased agriculture production around the world, especially from the late 1960s. The initiatives, led by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Borlaug" title="Norman Borlaug">Norman Borlaug</a> and credited with saving over a billion people from starvation, involved the development of high-yielding varieties of cereal grains, expansion of irrigation infrastructure, modernization of management techniques, distribution of hybridized seeds, synthetic fertilizers, and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> to farmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hazell_2009_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hazell_2009-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Synthetic nitrogen, along with mined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_phosphate" title="Rock phosphate">rock phosphate</a>, pesticides and mechanization, have greatly increased crop yields in the early 20th century. Increased supply of <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grains</a> has led to cheaper livestock as well. Further, global yield increases were experienced later in the 20th century when high-yield varieties of common staple grains such as rice, wheat, and corn were introduced as a part of the Green Revolution. The Green Revolution exported the technologies (including pesticides and synthetic nitrogen) of the developed world to the developing world. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> famously predicted that the Earth would not be able to support its growing population, but technologies such as the Green Revolution have allowed the world to produce a surplus of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BumperCrop_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-BumperCrop-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Green Revolution at first significantly increased rice yields in Asia, yield then levelled off. The genetic "yield potential" has increased for wheat, but the yield potential for rice has not increased since 1966, and the yield potential for maize has "barely increased in 35 years". It takes only a decade or two for herbicide-resistant weeds to emerge, and insects become resistant to insecticides within about a decade, delayed somewhat by crop rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tilman2002_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tilman2002-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2032" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg/220px-TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg/330px-TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg/440px-TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TONY_OWEN_ORGANIC_FARM_-_NARA_-_543017.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An organic farmer, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, 1972</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_agriculture">Organic agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Organic agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">Organic farming</a></div>
<p>For most of its history, agriculture has been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_agriculture" title="Organic agriculture">organic</a>, without synthetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilisers" title="Fertilisers">fertilisers</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a>, and without <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GMOs" title="GMOs">GMOs</a>. With the advent of chemical agriculture, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a> called for farming without synthetic pesticides, and his Agriculture Course of 1924 laid the foundation for <a href="/wiki/Biodynamic_agriculture" title="Biodynamic agriculture">biodynamic agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Northbourne" title="Lord Northbourne">Lord Northbourne</a> developed these ideas and presented his manifesto of <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> in 1940. This became a worldwide movement, and organic farming is now practiced in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
